            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1fa6d7f97c6744ccabcd9e76787afe82">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '309a178f-e163-41a9-bba0-6672bc12a35b'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d92532482" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold great promise for next-generation grid-scale energy storage. However, the highly instable electrolyte/electrode interphases threaten the long-term cycling of high-energy SIBs. In particular, the instable cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) at high voltage causes persistent electrolyte decomposition, transition metal dissolution, and fast capacity fade. Here, this work proposes a balanced principle for the molecular design of SIB electrolytes that enables an ultra-thin, homogeneous, and robust CEI layer by coupling an intrinsically oxidation-stable succinonitrile solvent with moderately solvating carbonates. The proposed electrolyte not only shows limited anodic decomposition thus leading to a thin CEI,&nbsp;but also suppresses dissolution of CEI components at high voltage. Consequently, the tamed electrolyte/electrode interphases enable extremely stable cycling of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F (NVOPF) cathodes with outstanding capacity retention (&gt;90%) over 3000 cycles (8 months) at 1 C with a high charging voltage of 4.3 V. Further, the NVOPF||hard carbon full cell shows stable cycling over 500 cycles at 1 C with a high average Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.6%. The electrolyte also endows high-voltage operation of SIBs with great temperature adaptability from −25 to 60&nbsp;°C, shedding light on the essence of fundamental electrolyte design for SIBs operating under harsh conditions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310051-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310051-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium is low-cost and naturally abundant and thus makes sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) promising for grid-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0001_R_d92532474e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#adma202310051-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0003_R_d92532474e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both the academia and industrial organizations have attached great importance to SIBs, and are actively seeking to leverage the established lithium-ion battery facilities for scaled production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0004_R_d92532474e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Joule">4</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0005_R_d92532474e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chin. Phys. Lett.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, high-energy polyanion cathodes, like Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2+2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and the Prussian analogs Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, as well as layered metal oxide materials, have found their routes for scalable production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0004_R_d92532474e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Joule">4</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0006_R_d92532474e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0007_R_d92532474e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In stark contrast to the prosperity of developing cathodes, the development of state-of-the-art electrolytes based on carbonate esters is far behind and fails to complementarily satisfy the harsh requirements for high-voltage SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0008_R_d92532474e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0009_R_d92532474e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As such, access to long-cycling life and wide-temperature compatibility remains a major concern, mainly due to the instability of the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) with notorious dissolution and poor self-healing capability at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0010_R_d92532474e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the solid electrolyte interphase on the anode (SEI) also faces the challenge of dissolution in the electrolyte. To bring SIBs a step closer to industrialization, it stands great significance to design electrolytes that meet the urgent needs for stable interphase formation at high voltage.</p>
         
         <p>To realize such an overarching goal, the electrolyte should allow manipulated anodic decompositions on the cathode surface in terms of the voltage at which it decomposes, the extent and products of its decomposition, as well as the dissolution behavior of the decomposed products,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0011_R_d92532474e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy">11</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0012_R_d92532474e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in addition to the basic properties of a wide liquid range and sufficient ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0013_R_d92532474e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For cells operating at high voltage, a CEI layer with a continuous, thin, and compact structure, while being insoluble in the electrolyte, holds the key to effectively inhibiting persistent parasitic reactions at the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0014_R_d92532474e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, conventional carbonate esters show low oxidation stability, strong solvation for the CEI, and thus poor passivation capability for charged cathodes at &gt;4&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0008_R_d92532474e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0010_R_d92532474e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> greatly hindering the formation of stable CEI.</p>
         
         <p>Recent efforts on improving high-voltage carbonate electrolytes have seen great progress in stabilizing the CEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0010_R_d92532474e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0014_R_d92532474e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett.">14</a>-<a href="#adma202310051-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0016_R_d92532474e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Utilizing electrolyte additives that sacrificially decompose on the cathode surface has been shown effective in forming a protective CEI layer. For instance, lithium difluorobis(oxalato) phosphate was adopted as a multifunctional additive to tame the solvation structure for stable and robust interphases, enabling a Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> battery to run for 220 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0015_R_d92532474e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The dynamic consumption of the additive may have hindered long-term interfacial stability and limited the cycling life as the CEI undergoes repeated formation, damage, and repair.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0017_R_d92532474e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The decomposition of the sodium bis(fuorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) salt reportedly enhanced the CEI stability of NaNi<sub>0.68</sub>Mn<sub>0.22</sub>Co<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (charged at 4.2&nbsp;V) with reduced electrolyte decomposition, structural reconstruction, and transition metal dissolution, rendering 89% capacity retention after 500 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0010_R_d92532474e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A low-concentration electrolyte with 0.3&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> afforded a stable anion-tuned and solvent-derived interphase formed on the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> cathode, resulting in high-rate capability with 100&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> capacity at 100 C under 55 °C for 300 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0018_R_d92532474e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Still, the battery lifetime is far from being practical for large-scale energy storage. The efforts to enhance the cycle life have also been focused on classical ion doping or constituent optimization of the cathodes to reduce the server phase evolution in cathode materials with restricted charging voltage (e.g., the layered oxides).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0003_R_d92532474e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Constructing a robust CEI by succinic anhydride (SA) and FEC has also shown the potential to mitigate the server phase evolution by preventing the transition metal dissolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0009_R_d92532474e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, only a few reports have worked on electrolyte design to enhance the wide-temperature capability or high compatibility with different electrode materials that are critical for practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0013_R_d92532474e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature">13</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0018_R_d92532474e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, salient efforts to invent weakly solvating electrolytes have restricted SEI dissolution and improved the stability of high-voltage SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0011_R_d92532474e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy">11</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0019_R_d92532474e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, rational taming of the formation, morphology, and dissolution of CEI is paramount but still lacking, leading to a grand challenge for the long cycling life of high-voltage SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a balanced electrolyte design principle wherein an intrinsically oxidation-stable succinonitrile (SN) solvent is coupled with a moderate-polarity diethyl carbonate (DEC) to prevent extensive anodic decomposition and to restrict CEI dissolution. SN molecules show high oxidation stability (a prerequisite for high-voltage cells), and are nonflammable and thermally stable (boiling point, 266 °C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0020_R_d92532474e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ideally, the polarity of the electrolyte should be low enough to reduce the dissolution of the CEI and also the SEI on the anode. Given the relatively high polarity of SN (dielectric constant, <i>ɛ</i> = 54),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0020_R_d92532474e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we further utilize a DEC co-solvent with an appropriate polarity (<i>ɛ</i> = 2.8),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0021_R_d92532474e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which as we show regulates the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure and maintains a robust SN-derived nitrogen-rich CEI layer, while also reducing the amount of free SN molecules in the electrolyte and leading to less dissolution of the CEI. In addition, SN possesses a high ratio of nitrile to alkyl group, thus showing strong coordination capability with the transition metals on the cathode surface and preventing their dissolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0020_R_d92532474e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0022_R_d92532474e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive is also introduced to generate fluoride-containing interphases and to reduce interphase deterioration. The proposed electrolyte containing 1&nbsp;mol sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) salt in a solvent mixture of SN:DEC:FEC (47.5:47.5:5 in volume) affords long cycling stability of a high-voltage Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F cathode (NVOPF, a plateau voltage at about 4&nbsp;V) with &gt;90% capacity retention over 3000 cycles (8 months) with a high charging voltage of 4.3&nbsp;V. An ultra-thin and uniform CEI layer with N-rich inorganic/organic components is formed on the NVOPF cathode surface. Further, the NVOPF||hard carbon full cell shows stable cycling over 500 cycles at 1 C with a high average Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.6%.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310051-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310051-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Electrolyte Design Principle</h3>
            
            <p>The basic criteria of solvents for high-voltage and wide-temperature-range SIB electrolytes include high oxidation stability and wide liquid range (i.e., low freezing point and high boiling point), as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1a,b</a>. Conventional carbonate electrolytes often show substantial decomposition at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0016_R_d92532474e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0023_R_d92532474e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Energy">23</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0024_R_d92532474e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the perspective of thermodynamics, nitrile solvent molecules show high intrinsic oxidation stability but have not been explored for any high-voltage SIBs. We thus propose to use SN as the main solvent to replace the conventionally used carbonate solvents. Meanwhile, as SN has a high melting point of more than 50 °C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0025_R_d92532474e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Cryst. Growth">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we propose to use DEC, a molecule with moderate polarity and low freezing point (−74.3 °C),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0021_R_d92532474e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as a co-solvent to improve the low-temperature kinetics. For comparison, dimethyl carbonate (DMC), molecularly similar to DEC, is also studied as a co-solvent but apparently fails to enable wide-temperature operation due to its limited liquid range (melting point of 4.6 °C; boiling point of 91 °C),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0021_R_d92532474e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as discussed below.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/380cc224-b16e-4642-97c2-ab40d1cad7b2/adma202310051-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/380cc224-b16e-4642-97c2-ab40d1cad7b2/adma202310051-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5a2a2501-ec72-4197-8a22-44b62aef36d8/adma202310051-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/380cc224-b16e-4642-97c2-ab40d1cad7b2/adma202310051-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrolyte design for taming robust CEI with suppressed dissolution for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries. a) Illustration of the continuous processes of CEI reformation and electrolyte decomposition due to the fragile and dissolution nature of the CEI layer in conventional carbonate electrolytes. b) Illustration of constructing a highly stable CEI layer and adaptability to wide temperature range operation using the designed electrolytes. c,d,g,h) Solvation structure of the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte as simulated by <i>ab</i> <i>initio</i> molecular dynamics: a snapshot of the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte molecular system (c), a representative Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure (d), coordination number distribution (g), and the quantified amount of the free SN molecules showing manipulation of the solvation structure (h). e) Calculated LUMO and HOMO energy levels of a single solvent molecule and the representative solvating complexes between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents in different electrolytes. f) Raman spectra of the SN-based solvents and the NaClO<sub>4</sub>-based electrolytes. i) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra showing the varied Na<sup>+</sup> coordination environments in different electrolytes. j) Electrochemical oxidation stability of different electrolytes as evaluated by linear cycling voltammetry measurement using stainless steel as the working electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Second, the electrolyte ought to enable thin yet robust interphase layers on the electrodes, with minimal dissolution and preferentially self-healing characteristics over long-term cycling. Carbonates are strong Lewis bases and strongly solvate with Na<sup>+</sup> (shown below by the nuclear magnetic resonance [NMR] studies) and may lead to severe CEI dissolution. We further anticipate that the presence of excessive free SN molecules with high polarity may induce CEI dissolution during repeated cycles, albeit it may beneficially enable N-rich interphase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0022_R_d92532474e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0026_R_d92532474e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochim. Acta">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We therefore utilize the balancing co-solvent, DEC, with a relatively low dielectric constant to tune the solvation structure of the electrolyte and to ensure low solvating capability for the interphase components. To demonstrate this principle, we prepared the following three electrolytes. One with only the main solvent of SN, that is, 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/SN:FEC (95:5 in vol, named SN-FEC), one with the main solvents of SN and DEC, 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/DEC:SN:FEC (47.5:47.5:5 in vol, named DEC-SN-FEC), and the baseline carbonate electrolyte, 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/ethylene carbonate (EC):propylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 in vol, named EC-PC). The solvation structures of these three electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1c,d,f–i</a>) are discussed in detail below.</p>
            
            <p>Finally, the anodic and cathodic decomposition of the electrolyte on electrode surfaces needs to be tamed toward forming mechanically and chemically robust interphase layers via tuning the solvation structure. Given the high polarity of SN, we introduce 5% FEC to facilitate fluoride-containing protective interphase layers. We anticipate that in contrast to the substantial solvent decomposition for EC-PC electrolytes forming a thick and uneven CEI layer, the SN-derived N-rich CEI is robust, thin, and homogeneous. Indeed, our quantum calculations show that the results of <i>ab</i> <i>initio</i> molecular dynamics (AIMD) trajectories point toward a potentially SN- and DEC-derived CEI in DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte, and a carbonate-derived CEI in EC-PC electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1e</a>). This will be further discussed below.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrolyte Solvation Structure</h3>
            
            <p>We performed Raman spectroscopy and <sup>23</sup>Na NMR measurements to study the solvation structure of the electrolytes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1f</a>, the C≡N band in SN shifts to a lower Raman wavenumber upon mixing with DEC, from 2256 to 2254 cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that SN shows strong intermolecular interaction with DEC and may lose its plastic crystal nature. Further, the deconvolution of C≡N bands in the electrolyte solutions of SN-FEC and DEC-SN-FEC shows an additional peak at 2264 cm<sup>−1</sup> (green peak), indicating the participation of SN in Na<sup>+</sup> solvation by C≡N···Na<sup>+</sup> coordination.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0027_R_d92532474e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0028_R_d92532474e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The observation is further supported by <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1i</a>). The downfield shift (more positive) of the <sup>23</sup>Na chemical shift in SN-FEC compared to the EC-PC electrolyte implies weaker solvation for Na<sup>+</sup> by SN molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0029_R_d92532474e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0030_R_d92532474e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, the upfield shift of <sup>23</sup>Na chemical shift in DEC-SN-FEC compared to SN-FEC again confirms participation of both SN and DEC in Na<sup>+</sup> solvation. Note that the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation strength which in turn determines the interphase components is related to the electron donicity, and not the dielectric constant of the solvents, whereas the dissolution of interphases is related to both electron donicity and dielectric constant of the solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0011_R_d92532474e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy">11</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0012_R_d92532474e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The solvation structures of the three electrolytes were further revealed by the radial distribution function (RDFs, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c,g</a>, Supporting Information) and coordination number analyses obtained from the AIMD trajectory (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d,h</a>, Supporting Information). Two dominant peaks of the RDFs for Na─N (at 2.40 Å) and Na─O (at 2.35 Å) appear for DEC-SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information), in comparison to Na─N (at 2.4 Å) for SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information) and Na─O (at 2.30 Å) for EC-PC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2g</a>, Supporting Information). These results imply that both SN and DEC are involved in solvating NaClO<sub>4</sub>. In detail, DEC and SN molecules occupy the first Na<sup>+</sup> solvation shell with coordination numbers of 2.16 and 1.85 for N and O for DEC-SN-FEC, respectively, indicating a higher fraction of SN than DEC in the solvation shell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0031_R_d92532474e861" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1h</a> shows a lower free SN molecule/salt ratio of 3.7:1 for DEC-SN-FEC compared to 6.7:1 for SN-FEC, in alignment with our expectation of a reduced amount of free SN molecules which in turn benefits less CEI dissolution. Furthermore, a lower free solvent/salt ratio of 6.3:1 is also obtained for DEC-SN-FEC compared to 9.7:1 and 7.3:1 for EC-PC and SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Further, the percentage of free SN and solvent in electrolytes is also shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c,d</a> (Supporting Information) for comparison.</p>
            
            <p>The HOMO energy values of the solvent molecules and typical Na<sup>+</sup> complexes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1e</a>) were calculated using density functional theory (DFT) calculations. Upon observing the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure which in fact determines the decomposition in the inner Helmholtz plane of the interface,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0032_R_d92532474e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">32</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0033_R_d92532474e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the HOMO energy level lies on the Na<sup>+</sup>-(DEC-SN-FEC)-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> complex (−7.68&nbsp;eV), lower than that of the free solvent molecules (e.g., DEC, −8.05&nbsp;eV; SN, −9.53&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1e</a>), indicating a potentially SN- and DEC-derived CEI layer in DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0009_R_d92532474e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For comparison, the HOMO energy level of the baseline electrolyte lies on that of the Na<sup>+</sup>-EC-PC-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> complex, suggesting a carbonate-derived CEI layer in the EC-PC electrolyte. We further assess the anodic stability of the three electrolytes by linear scan voltammetry at a scan rate of 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1j</a>); the SN-containing electrolytes exhibit high oxidation stability up to 4.8&nbsp;V, which is higher than that for the baseline carbonate electrolytes and sufficient for the upper limit of 4.3&nbsp;V used in this work.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>We now adopt the high-voltage and high-capacity NVOPF as the cathode material for demonstration. The NVOPF cathode material exhibits a stable crystalline framework with wide Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0034_R_d92532474e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the long-term cycling of such cathode remains challenging due to the dissolution/damage of the CEI layers under high-voltage conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0017_R_d92532474e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, the high-energy NVOPF cathode delivers a capacity of 125&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C with two obvious flat voltage plateaus at 3.6 and 4&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The cycling stability of the half cells, with NVOPF cathode and sodium metal anode, fitted with the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte significantly outperforms those with SN-FEC and EC-PC electrolytes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0002">2a–c</a>). The cell with EC-PC electrolyte exhibits fast capacity fading and a dramatic increase of cell polarization over long cycles at a rate of 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information). After 3000 cycles, its capacity retention is only 38.4% with a low average CE &lt; 98.7% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0002">2d,e</a>), and particularly the CE of the first 100 cycles is as low as 96.6% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The above observations indicate severe and continuous parasitic reactions occur due to the instable CEI in carbonates. The cell with SN-FEC electrolyte delivers much better cycling performance with 92.1% capacity retention during the first 1000 cycles. However, it shows fast capacity decay and accelerated cell overpotential increase in the subsequent cycles, which indicates the onset of CEI damage. In great contrast, the cell with our optimized DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte exhibits excellent long-term cycling performance, with 97.1% and 94.1% capacity retention over 1000 and 3000 cycles (≈230 days) at 1 C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0002">2d</a>), outperforming the reported cells' cycling lifespan on electrolyte design (see Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, for more details). Moreover, the almost overlapped charge/discharge profiles demonstrate a marginal increase of cell overpotential using DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte over cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0002">2c</a>), as is also evidenced by d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> profiles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to the frequently fluctuated CE of the cell in SN-FEC that indicates continuous CEI dissolution/repairing, the cell with DEC-SN-FEC shows a highly stable CE with an average value of 99.6% throughout the cycling. Similarly, the cell shows very stable cycling at a higher rate of 2 C, with 91.1% capacity retained over 3000 cycles for DEC-SN-FEC, much higher than that for the cells with SN-FEC (72.2%) and EC-PC (37.0%) electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). The bulk structure of the cycled NVOPF cathode is maintained (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the outstanding cycling stability is mainly attributed to the stable electrode interphases as discussed below. In addition, the robust CEI also contributes to a higher rate performance for the cell with DEC-SN-FEC over those using SN-FEC or EC-PC (further discussed below Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/792842e0-32ac-4fb0-bcea-14a9235720e0/adma202310051-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/792842e0-32ac-4fb0-bcea-14a9235720e0/adma202310051-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/525bb18f-26a1-4d5d-ae67-6a16890d332e/adma202310051-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/792842e0-32ac-4fb0-bcea-14a9235720e0/adma202310051-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Long-term cycling performance of NVOPF half cells at ambient temperature. a–c) Voltage profiles as a function of cycle number for the NVOPF half cells using EC-PC (a), SN-FEC (b), and DEC-SN-FEC (c) electrolytes. d,e) Long-term cycling performance of the NVOPF half cells at 1 C using different electrolytes after three conditioning cycles at 0.1 C (d), and the corresponding Coulombic efficiency (CE) (e). Note that the sudden fluctuation in capacity/CE shown in (d,e) is due to an unplanned increase in the temperature of the testing chamber.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition to the excellent performance permitted for the cathode, significant improvements in cycling stability are also achieved for the hard carbon anode with DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte over the referenced electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a>, Supporting Information). At 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell with EC-PC electrolyte demonstrates a lower capacity and rapid capacity fading over cycling due to the poorly stable SEI. The cell using SN-FEC shows obviously fast capacity fades over 1400 cycles owing to the deterioration of the SEI layer, as evidenced by the inferior Coulombic efficiency (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7b</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the cell using the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte shows excellent long cycling stability over 3500 cycles, with an average CE of up to 99.8%. Further, the HC cell with DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte delivers the highest capacity of 318.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with stable voltage polarization at the current density of 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c–e</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the sodium anode stability with Na|Na symmetric cells was assessed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The result shows relatively stable polarization for sodium stripping/plating with DEC-SN-FEC compared to the ones with SN-FEC and EC-PC. This indicates that the suppressed side reactions at the anode interface by adopting DEC-SN-FEC also contribute slightly to the stable cycling of NVOPF half cells.</p>
            
            <p>We further evaluated the cell performances at a wider range of temperatures. At a lower temperature down to −25 °C, the cell with the SN-FEC electrolyte fails to operate due to the solidification of the electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). In sharp contrast, the cell with the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte shows a capacity of 115&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, retaining 92% of that at 25 °C, superior to the baseline cell with EC-PC that shows a capacity of about 70&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and a larger cell polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3a,b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, a higher initial CE is shown for the cell using DEC-SN-FEC, indicating high reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in NVOPF owed to the advantageous Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion property of the CEI at low temperature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8f38fad-9bf4-446c-9bbd-314910268833/adma202310051-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8f38fad-9bf4-446c-9bbd-314910268833/adma202310051-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/59d57a93-1783-4299-a0af-f170b70d2368/adma202310051-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8f38fad-9bf4-446c-9bbd-314910268833/adma202310051-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance evaluation under other conditions. a,b) Cycling performance of the NVOPF half cells (a) and the corresponding voltage profiles (b) at 0.1 C under −25 °C, demonstrating higher capacity retention and smaller voltage polarization for the cell with DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte. c) Cycling performance of the NVOPF half cells at 2 C under 60 °C. d) The cycling performance of the NVOPF||HC full cells using different electrolytes at 1 C under 25 °C. e) Rate capabilities of the NVOPF||HC full cells and the prolonged cycling performance at 0.5 C under 25 °C; the N/P ratio for the full cells is kept at about 1.3 and the C-rate is based on the mass of NVOPF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, we also demonstrate the compatibility and advantages of DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte at a higher charging voltage of 4.5&nbsp;V by using a layer metal oxide cathode Na<sub>2/3</sub>Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (named NNMO, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The cells show stable cycling with high capacity retention of 91.7% at 25 °C (at 0.3 C) and 95.3% at −25 °C (at 0.1 C) over 100 cycles in DEC-SN-FEC, both of which are much higher than those in baseline electrolyte (75.8% and 70.3%). Note that although the baseline EC-PC electrolyte exhibits a higher ionic conductivity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), the cell still shows inferior performance than that using DEC-SN-FEC (especially the long-term stability and low-temperature performance), confirming the importance of interphase stability.</p>
            
            <p>At a high temperature of 60 °C, the NVOPF cell with the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte shows a higher capacity of up to 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 60 °C with stable cycling over 500 cycles compared to that in EC-PC and SN-FEC electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3c</a>), suggesting the interphases are robust enough to mitigate electrolyte oxidation at high temperature. Replacing DEC with DMC (melting point of 4.6 °C; boiling point of 91 °C) as the co-solvent, the cell with DMC-SN-FEC electrolyte can only operate 15 cycles at 60 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating using a co-solvent with wide liquid range is the premise for wide-temperature operation. We further studied ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) as a co-solvent to demonstrate the balanced electrolyte design principle. EMC is close to DEC in both the melting point (−53 °C) and boiling point (110 °C),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0021_R_d92532474e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the cell performance at low or high temperatures is much inferior to the one with DEC-SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the robust interphase layer associated with the ion solvation structure for DEC-SN-FEC holds the key to stability at all temperatures.</p>
            
            <p>To better represent practical conditions, full cells using NVOPF cathodes and HC anodes were evaluated for the three electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the cycling stability of NVOPF||HC full cells over 500 cycles at 1 C, in which an average CE of 99.6% is retained in the cell with the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte, outperforming the cells with SN-FEC (98.4%) and EC-PC (99.5%) electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information). During the initial 50 cycles, a high average CE of 99.2% is achieved in the full cell with DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte, higher than that in the cells with SN-FEC (98.4%) and EC-PC (98.7%) electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3d</a>). This indicates a more stable cell interphase and higher compatibility of the full cell with DEC-SN-FEC and is responsible for its higher delivered capacity. Further, the full cells with DEC-SN-FEC present superior rate capability, as well as enhanced cycling stability at 0.5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0003">3e</a>). Correspondingly, an obviously superior CE in a wide range of current densities from 0.1 to 15 C is achieved for the DEC-SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), showcased by the high average CE of 99.5% at 0.5 C in contrast to the 98.3% and 98.1% in EC-PC and SN-FEC electrolytes. The designed DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte also endows NVOPF||HC full cells with decreased voltage polarization due to the robust CEI/SEI (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Overall, all the electrochemical results above prove the superiority of DEC-SN-FEC over the two counterparts and point to an utterly different interphase at the electrodes.</p>
            
            <p>Efforts of additive engineering have been devoted to exploiting the high-voltage electrolytes in SIBs for promoting cycling stability. However, merely relying on the consumed additive (with a limited amount ≤ 5%) seems to be insufficient to maintain stable interphases over a long lifespan. The advanced localized high-concentration electrolytes and weakly solvating electrolytes have shown significant progress in boosting high-voltage SIBs from the aspect of either interphase regulation or SEI dissolution; but unfortunately, very few studies have focused on the CEI damage problems, leading to a grand challenge on long-term operation under harsh conditions. Compared to the previous studies, our work demonstrates the significance of CEI dissolution, and fulfills the comprehensive performance evaluation including wide-temperature capability, different cell configurations from half cells to full cells, and high compatibility with various electrode materials, showing the great potential for practical application.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0060-title">2.4 Characterizations of the Interphases</h3>
            
            <p>The morphology and compositions of the CEI on the NVOPF cathode were systematically investigated using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). As revealed by the HR-TEM image, an uneven and thick CEI layer is observed on the NVOPF surface after 300 cycles in the EC-PC electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4a</a>). In contrast, a thinner albeit rough CEI layer is observed for the SN-FEC electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4b</a>), reflecting inhibited electrolyte decomposition and CEI growth with SN as the main solvent. Furthermore, a uniform and thin CEI layer is observed for the DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4c</a>), demonstrating the importance of DEC co-solvent to minimize the damage/dissolution of the CEI.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a1b9db3-610e-4de5-9db5-2b81aae0bb5c/adma202310051-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a1b9db3-610e-4de5-9db5-2b81aae0bb5c/adma202310051-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/54a4902e-19a6-49b3-aa79-ead4f1f760cc/adma202310051-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a1b9db3-610e-4de5-9db5-2b81aae0bb5c/adma202310051-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of CEI layers on NVOPF cathodes after 300 cycles. a–c) HR-TEM images showing the CEI layer formed in EC-PC (a), SN-FEC (b), and DEC-SN-FEC (c) electrolytes.&nbsp;Orange dashed lines are used to outline the CEI layer on the&nbsp;cycled NVOPF cathodes. d–g) XPS results of the CEI components on the cycled NVOPF cathodes in different electrolytes: XPS spectra of N 1s (d), F 1s (e), Na 1s (f) and quantified atomic ratios of the elements in CEI layer from DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte with the increasing sputtering time (g). The N 1s and F 1s spectra in DEC-SN-FEC electrolytes with sputtering are also displayed in columns of the corresponding depth profiling.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The composition of the CEI layers on cycled NVOPF cathodes was analyzed using XPS and is correlated with its potential dissolution/damage behavior. We first discuss the surface CEI, that is, without ion-sputtering for XPS measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4d–g</a>). First, the C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s spectra are fully different from those of the fresh NVOPF electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). In the baseline EC-PC electrolyte, the decomposition of carbonate solvents (EC and PC) produces a relatively Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>-rich CEI layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0035_R_d92532474e1196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as reflected by the Na 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4f</a>). Note that the observation of F for the cell with the fluorine-free EC-PC electrolyte originates from the structural degradation of NVOPF cathodes at high voltage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0036_R_d92532474e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as discussed below. In the SN-based electrolytes, the surface CEI layer contains more insoluble Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaF components (shown in N 1s and F 1s, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4d,e</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0012_R_d92532474e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0022_R_d92532474e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0037_R_d92532474e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Environ. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which are apparently derived from the decomposition of SN and FEC. Besides, the C─N peak (instead of the C─O peak) prevails in the C 1s spectra for both DEC-SN-FEC and SN-FEC electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0038_R_d92532474e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Power Sources">38</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0039_R_d92532474e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Joule">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results demonstrate an N-rich CEI layer (Na<sub>3</sub>N and organic C-N groups), which tends to be less insoluble in the electrolyte, as evidenced by the minimal loss in capacity discussed below.</p>
            
            <p>Organic CEI species tend to show a higher solubility in electrolytes and thus lower high-voltage stability than inorganic NaF/Na<sub>3</sub>N.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0012_R_d92532474e1236" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0013_R_d92532474e1236" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to SN-FEC, the CEI in DEC-SN-FEC contains less C─N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4d</a>), and less organic components (statistically represented by C), as well as reduced ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion decomposition (Cl 2p spectra, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a>, Supporting Information). These observations suggest that the presence of DEC in the solvation leads to a CEI with more insoluble components (Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaF) and/or reduced dissolution of CEI, which is responsible for the steady cell operation at high voltage.</p>
            
            <p>Depth profiling of the CEI was further performed by Ar<sub>n</sub><sup>+</sup> clusters sputtering to further elaborate the element distribution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4d,e,g</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16–S19</a>, Supporting Information). For the EC-PC electrolyte, with increased sputtering time, the fraction of organic species in the CEI slightly decreases (based on the C atom ratio) while the inorganic species increases (based on Na atom ratio, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), indicating an inhomogeneous CEI along the thickness. On the contrary, for the SN-FEC and DEC-SN-FEC electrolytes, most inorganic species in the CEI (i.e., Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaF) show uniform distribution along the thickness (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4d,e</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), indicating a chemically uniform CEI layer. Note that the CEI in DEC-SN-FEC persistently shows a higher fraction of F and N, and a lower fraction of C than that in SN-FEC at all sputtered depths (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0004">4g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information). As such, we conclude that the inorganic-rich and relatively homogenous CEI formed in DEC-SN-FEC not only alleviates the continuous CEI damage/dissociation and electrolyte decomposition but also offers desirable mechanical stability upon high-voltage cycling.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0070-title">2.5 Analysis of CEI Dissolution</h3>
            
            <p>We further quantified the extent of CEI dissolution by its impact on capacity loss when a cycled cell is paused at an open circuit so that CEI dissolution occurs if any.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0011_R_d92532474e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cycled NVOPF cells were paused at 4.3&nbsp;V (the fully charged state) after every 15 cycles at 1 C for <i>x</i> h (<i>x</i> = 25, 50, and 75), and then resumed cycling by following the original procedures. As exemplified by the pause of 75&nbsp;h, the cell with EC-PC exhibits a high average capacity loss (54.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0005">5a</a>), low capacity retention (43.4%, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0005">5b</a>) and high discharge voltage drop (0.7&nbsp;V, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0005">5c</a>) on the following discharge, in great contrast to that for SN-FEC (27.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 74.3%, 0.32&nbsp;V) and DEC-SN-FEC (20.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 80.8%, 0.3&nbsp;V). These results show that the cells with DEC-SN-FEC and SN-FEC exhibit less energy loss after each pause as compared to the EC-PC counterpart (shown by the colored area, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0005">5c,d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), apparently meaning less anodic decomposition of electrolyte, less CEI dissolution/repair, and/or less vanadium dissolution. Further, the energy loss upon pause after 1000 cycles in DEC-SN-FEC is close to that paused at 45<sup>th</sup> cycles, and significantly lower than that in EC-PC and SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), also evidencing its capability for suppressing CEI dissolution in long-term cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a2ca4d0-5ce0-433b-bfcc-bd607e0cc547/adma202310051-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a2ca4d0-5ce0-433b-bfcc-bd607e0cc547/adma202310051-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0a58486-50f6-49c3-8126-45c3593b55b1/adma202310051-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a2ca4d0-5ce0-433b-bfcc-bd607e0cc547/adma202310051-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Analysis of CEI dissolution by capacity loss obtained from the cycled NVOPF cells paused for different durations. a,b) Comparison of the capacity loss (a) and capacity retention (b) for each pause of the cells in EC-PC, SN-FEC, and DEC-SN-FEC electrolytes. Each test has two cells in parallel for statistical considerations, and the capacity retention (<i>R</i>) and the capacity loss (<i>Q</i><sub>loss</sub>) are obtained from the capacity delivered before and after each pause (e.g., <i>R</i>45<sup>st</sup> = <i>Q</i>discharge, 44<sup>st</sup>/<i>Q</i>discharge, 45<sup>th</sup> and <i>Q</i>loss, 45<sup>st</sup> = <i>Q</i>discharge, 44<sup>st</sup> − <i>Q</i>discharge, 45<sup>th</sup>). c,d) The voltage profiles before and after 75 h pause at the end of the 44<sup>th</sup> charge for the cells using EC-PC (c) and DEC-SN-FEC (d) electrolytes. The colored areas between the 44<sub>th</sub> and 45<sup>th</sup> discharged voltage profiles represent the energy loss; note that the two charging profiles in (c,d) remain unchanged (i.e., overlapped here).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Vanadium and phosphorus dissolution has been identified as one of the other reasons for NPVOF failure at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0036_R_d92532474e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Storage Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is here experimentally confirmed by detecting vanadium and phosphorus in the separators and anodes from cells after 300 cycles in EC-PC electrolyte (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information, and discussion below Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The dissolution of vanadium and phosphorus is suppressed in the SN-FEC and DEC-SN-FEC electrolytes due to the synergistic effect of SN showing strong coordination capability with surface vanadium<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0020_R_d92532474e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the high mechanical stability of CEI.</p>
            
            <p>The interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub>) of NVOPF half cells was also quantified by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurements. Owning to the robust electrode interphases, the <i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub> values of the cycled NVOPF cathodes in DEC-SN-FEC are only about 30–40% of that in EC-PC and SN-FEC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The cell with DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte also shows smaller cell resistance (<i>R</i><sub>cell</sub>, including <i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub> and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>)) after 900 and 1400 cycles. Further discussions are provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) with related discussion in Supporting Information.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202310051-sec-0080-title">2.6 Analysis of SEI on Hard Carbon</h3>
            
            <p>We further analyzed the nature of SEI on hard carbon anodes to account for the superior cycling performance of NVOPF||HC full cells in DEC-SN-FEC. First, the HR-TEM image of cycled HC anodes demonstrates the formation of a thinner (about 9–12&nbsp;nm) and thickness-homogeneous SEI layer in DEC-SN-FEC compared to that of SN-FEC and EC-PC electrolytes (with a thickness of about 4–17&nbsp;nm, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6a–c</a>). Surprisingly, the XPS analyses of the SEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6d–f</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25–S27</a>, Supporting Information) show there is a limited amount of N-containing species in the SEI for both DEC-SN-FEC and SN-FEC (N 1s spectra and its depth profiling, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a,b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating restricted SN cathodic decomposition. This is consistent with the calculated lowest LUMO level of Na<sup>+</sup>-(FEC)-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> complex that indicates a preferential reduction of FEC on anode surface forming fluoride-rich SEI (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0001">1e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). Indeed, the SEI contains a large fraction of NaF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6d,f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202310051-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebe356a9-360d-4f36-9b6e-ff552d7d3f39/adma202310051-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebe356a9-360d-4f36-9b6e-ff552d7d3f39/adma202310051-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d35c8d2-4036-4750-ba1f-9fdc741285ae/adma202310051-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebe356a9-360d-4f36-9b6e-ff552d7d3f39/adma202310051-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202310051-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202310051" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of SEI layers on HC anodes after 250 cycles. a–c) HR-TEM images of SEI layers formed in DEC-SN-FEC (a), SN-FEC (b), and EC-PC (c) electrolytes.&nbsp;Orange dashed lines are used to outline the SEI layer on the&nbsp;cycled HC anode. d–f) XPS results of the SEI components on the cycled HC anodes with increasing sputtering time: the quantified atomic ratios of the elements in the SEI layer in different electrolytes: F and Na atoms (d), C and O atoms (e), and XPS spectra of F 1s in the HC cell using DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte (f).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Note that the fraction of NaF in SEI for DEC-SN-FEC is in fact lower than that for SN-FEC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6d</a>). Further, the SEI in DEC-SN-FEC shows a more intense C─C/C─H peak<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0015_R_d92532474e1515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0039_R_d92532474e1515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Joule">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and a higher total ratio of C atoms at the outer layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the reduction of both DEC and FEC molecules. Further, as the etching depth increases, we observe an increased total Na ratio (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6d</a>) and fraction of NaF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6f</a>), as well as a decreased total O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6e</a>). These results suggest an outer SEI layer rich in organics species (benefiting volume buffer), and an inner SEI layer rich in inorganic NaF (yielding mechanical integrity and electron insulation to suppress SEI growth). This apparently results from the co-decomposition of DEC and FEC solvents. Meanwhile, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in the cell using SN-FEC is also lowered from 985.42 to 559.67 Ω along with a smaller <i>R</i><sub>interphase</sub> after solvating with DEC solvent (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, with further discussion in Supporting Information). We further note that the SEI formed in EC-PC contains a very low fraction of organics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6e</a>) and a large fraction of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202310051-fig-0006">6d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26a,b</a>, Supporting Information).
