The excessive Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> reportedly causes a high-pH environment in the vicinity of the electrode/binder surface, possibly inducing the generation of undesired HF that repeatedly deteriorates the interphase and consumes the electrolyte (see Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27c</a>, Supporting Information, for more details).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0040_R_d92532474e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#adma202310051-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0042_R_d92532474e1570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310051-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310051-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we reported a balanced electrolyte design for high-voltage SIBs. Our rational solvent design coupling an intrinsically oxidation-stable succinonitrile solvent with a moderately solvating carbonate suppresses the dissolution/damage of CEI at high voltage. This is achieved via regulated Na<sup>+</sup> solvation that reduces the fraction of free SN molecules and tames SN-derived CEI components with low solubility in the electrolyte. The designed DEC-SN-FEC electrolyte enables the NVOPF||HC full cell with high cycling stability. Remarkably, the NVOPF half cells not only show wide-temperature operation (−25 to 60 °C) but also retain &gt;90% of capacity after 8 months' cycling with an average CE of over 99.6% at 25 °C. This design strategy highlights the essence of fundamental electrolyte design for SIBs operating under harsh conditions. With the establishment of the CEI dissolution-cell performance correlation, it may offer new possibilities in the electrolyte design space for enhancing stable high-energy and wide-temperature SIBs. The economic advantages of SIBs make them practical for large-scale energy storage applications and for such, the long cycling life and wide temperature adaptability are the key. Based on the scalability of low-cost SN and DEC, our work offers an opportunity to resolve the commercial concerns (i.e., manufacture cost and overall sustainability), and expedites the industrialization of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310051-sec-0100">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310051-sec-0100-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0110">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0120">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310051-sec-0130-title"> Electrolytes and Electrodes Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>Battery-grade NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt was purchased from Canrd New Energy Technology Co., LTD and dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 24&nbsp;h before use. Battery-grade EC, PC, FEC, DEC, and DMC were purchased from TCI and dried with 4 A molecular sieves (Sigma-Aldrich) for &gt;48 h in the Ar-filled glovebox before use. Succinonitrile (&gt;98%) was provided from Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd and dried with 4 A molecular sieves before use. Electrolytes were prepared in the Ar-filled glove box with O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1&nbsp;ppm, where the NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt was dissolved with the designed solvent or solvent mixture. In detail, 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/EC:PC (1:1 in vol), 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/SN:FEC (95:5 in vol) and 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/DEC:SN:FEC (47.5:47.5:5 in vol) were prepared, among which NaClO<sub>4</sub>/EC:PC was selected as the baseline electrolyte, and the 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/SN:FEC electrolyte was further optimized by introducing DEC solvent. The as-prepared electrolytes were kept for &gt;24 h in a glove box before being assembled into&nbsp;cells. The commercial HC powder was purchased from Kuraray Co. and used as received. The involved electrode materials and conductive carbons were vacuumed-dried at 100 °C overnight before making the slurry. HC anodes were prepared by coating a slurry containing HC:Super P:polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder (8:1:1, in weight) on Al foil. NVOPF cathode material was prepared based on a reported scalable synthesis route.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0043_R_d92532474e1623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Mater. Adv.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Briefly, a solution containing NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, NaF, NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O (with the molar ratio of V:P:F = 1:3:1.7) and citric acid was rapidly mixed with another solution containing VOSO<sub>4</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O to obtain the precursor solution at 30 °C. The precursor was filtered, washed in sequence with DI H<sub>2</sub>O and ethanol, and then dried overnight. The product was obtained by being treated with stable argon flow at 300–450 °C for 0.5 h. Cathodes were prepared by coating slurry with NVOPF:Ketjen Black:PVDF (7:2:1, in weight) on Al foil. Note that the NVOPF cathodes and Ketjen Black should be carefully mixed with an appropriate amount of NMP solvent to obtain an evenly dispersed slurry, and a suitable loading mass of 2–2.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−1</sup>. Both HC and NVOPF electrodes were dried overnight in a vacuum oven at 120 °C. After naturally cooling down to room temperature, the prepared electrodes were rapidly transferred to the glove box to avoid the influence of humidity. Finally, the electrodes and 150&nbsp;µL electrolyte were assembled into the 2025-type coin cell packages by using pure Na discs and glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) as counterparts and separators, respectively. The Na disc was used immediately after cutting from bulk Na metal (99.8%, Sigma-Aldrich).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310051-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The oxidation stability of the electrolytes was first evaluated by the linear sweeping voltammetry (LSV) on Gamry instruments (5000 E) with stainless steel as the working electrode (WE) and Na disc as both the reference and counter electrode. LSV was performed in the voltage range from the open circuit potential to 5.5&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at a scanning rate of 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. All cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box (O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1&nbsp;ppm) using a 2025-type coin-cell with an electrolyte amount of 150&nbsp;µL. The NVOPF and HC electrodes have the same geometric area (both 10&nbsp;mm in diameter), and the anode/cathode capacity loading ratio was about 1.3. All the coin cells were subjected to galvanostatic cycling on a multi-channel battery tester (Neware). The voltage ranges for NVOPF (1 C = 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and HC cells were 2–4.3 and 0–2.5&nbsp;V (<i>vs</i>. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). For the full cell, both NVOPF and HC electrodes were pre-sodiated in half cells (NVOPF||Na and Na||HC) for three cycles before they were assembled. The NVOPF||HC full cells were cycled between 1.2 and 4.1&nbsp;V (1 C = 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310051-sec-0150-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The post-cycling NVOPF/HC electrodes were disassembled in a glove box after 300/250 cycles for the post-mortem interphase characterization. The collected electrodes were first soaked in DMC for 1–2 min, washed with DMC four times to remove residual electrolytes, and then dried. Prior to further characterization, all electrodes were carefully sealed in airtight containers in the glove box. XPS spectra were recorded by Thermo Fisher Nexsa device, and the surface was sputtered using Ar<sub>n</sub><sup>+</sup> clusters for depth profiling investigation. C 1s peak was set to 284.8&nbsp;eV as the internal standard for calibration. The surface morphologies and interphase thickness were measured by transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Thermo Fisher Talos F200s). The dissolved V and P were determined by an inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometer (ICP-OES, Aglient 5110). The solvation of SN and its intermolecular interaction with DEC solvent were reflected by Raman spectra using a microscopic confocal Raman spectrometer (WiTech alpha 300R) with an excitation of 532&nbsp;nm laser light. The differences in the bonding environment of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolytes were detected by NMR (Bruker, 600&nbsp;MHz Ultrashield) using a coaxial NMR tube, and 1&nbsp;mol NaCl-D<sub>2</sub>O was adopted as an internal standard.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202310051-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202310051-sec-0160-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>The AIMD simulations were performed using the Vienna <i>ab</i> <i>initio</i> Simulation Package (VASP.5.4.4),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0044_R_d92532474e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">44</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0045_R_d92532474e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Phys. Rev. B">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> employing the projector-augmented wave (PAW) formalism. The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0046_R_d92532474e1710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was employed to describe the exchange-correlation energy. The long-range van der Waals (vdW) dispersion interactions were accounted for using the DFT-D3 method developed by Grimme and co-workers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0047_R_d92532474e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Chem. Phys.">47</a>, <a href="#adma202310051-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0048_R_d92532474e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Comput. Chem.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The plane-wave energy cutoff was set as 400&nbsp;eV, and the Brillouin zone was sampled at the Γ point only. Three systems, a-c 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/PC, 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub> in DEC/SN/FEC, and 1&nbsp;mol NaClO<sub>4</sub>/SN/FEC, containing 459, 406, and 378 atoms, respectively, were simulated. To achieve a simulation density close to the experimental measurements, the simulation boxes were set to cubes with side lengths of 17.13 Å for system a, 16.98 Å for system b, and 17.09 Å for system c. The temperature was set at 298 K, consistent with the experimental conditions. The systems were equilibrated at 298 K in the canonical ensemble (NVT)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0049_R_d92532474e1728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. A">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> for 100&nbsp;ps using a time step of 1 fs. The Nose–Hoover thermostat<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0050_R_d92532474e1733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Chem. Phys.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was employed to keep a constant temperature.</p>
                  
                  <p>The cluster calculations were carried out using Gaussian 16 package,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0051_R_d92532474e1741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the starting structures obtained from the AIMD calculations. The Becke three-parameter hybrid method combined with the Lee–Yang–Parr correlation functional (B3LYP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202310051-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202310051-bib-0052_R_d92532474e1746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Chem. Phys">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> at the 6–311+G(d, p) level was used to optimize geometries.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202310051-sec-0190">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202310051-sec-0190-title"> Author Contributions</h2>
         
         <p>Y.M.L. and Q.Q.P. designed the study. Y.M.L. conducted the experiment. L.J.Z. performed AIMD calculations and data analysis with the assistance from Y.A. E.H.W. and K.E.S. carried out the Raman, XPS, and HRTEM characterizations. All authors have thoroughly discussed the data and provided input at various stages. Y.M.L. and Q.Q.P. wrote and edited the manuscript with feedback from all the contributing authors. Q.Q.P. acted as the project supervisor.</p>
         </section>
